RATING: 5
A simple and fast commodity trading game, I think it works best in terms of it's size and requirements to learn, and ultimately you're not only trying to exploit the cards that show up as a set collection aspect, but also knowing that your opponent is working on similar sets. The camel piece is a nice touch and helps move the center market along. I do think having the most camels at the end is under valued though, but not by much. Maybe that's intentional, I don't know.

Ultimately though, it's not the quick game I'm most inclined to reach for, but it is fun when it does hit the table. I think the best of three rounds for scoring can drag, but it almost takes that long to get a feel for how the game is going to progress...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Been playing a lot of the Android version. I like the basic trading mechanism and the diminishing returns of the points but I think the bonus points and the luck of the draw are terrible. The variable bonus tiles make one point games seem like pointless affairs and I've seen plenty of 20+ point blowouts (definitely a few 40s and I think a 50) simply due to one player getting all the expensive goods. 

In fact, the luck of the draw is so pronounced that when one player gets a camel lock, you can easily get into a cycle where both players simply take the only card available, hoping to out-draw their opponent. The hardest level AI agrees with me on this so I feel pretty confident it's the correct play. If they take camels they don't touch my majority, if I take camels I am gaining no points and giving my opponent an opportunity to get a good group of cards. So here we sit, top-decking and hoping for high value cards, with score and timing meaning nothing.

I feel like a good game could be designed from the core mechanism, perhaps by normalizing the bonus tokens and by implementing a Power Grid style upcoming queue. 

Perfectly acceptable as a time-waster phone game but not something I would purchase to play physically. It's also hilariously overpriced for a deck of cards and some tokens. And I'm a guy that puts value on design over components.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It plays a little blah and random the first time through, but after one or two more plays the strategy comes through and that adds to the experience.

There's still practically no strategy to the game itself. It's purely a human-to-human guessing game about what the other person will do, and the game is just a mechanism for letting you prove that out one way or the other. And, in a sense, the fact that the game has distilled itself down to that level is fairly impressive. But ultimately the decisions are so simple that guessing right about what the other person will do lacks enough oomph for me. Even my mom felt that it seemed a bit overly-light.

That said, I bought this one because it looked like it had more to it than Lost Cities. So I guess, good thing I didn't buy that one as well!

RATING: 7
2

Excellent. Only one move per turn, so it plays quickly, but a lot of tricky decisions to make. You always have to be careful of what you're leaving your opponent and, other than the initial deal, you always can get some idea of what your opponent is trying to collect (and then just sneak in to grab the high tokens). It's really simple, quick, and challenging. I like it. It's not Innovation or Battle Line, but it's solid. (Though Innovation + expansion takes a loooong time to set up)

RATING: 8
Rules-wise, Jaipur is simplicity itself.  It's a game of set collection, with the various commodities you are collecting worth different amounts.  There are also substantial bonuses for large sets.  The catch is that while you are clogging your hand with a single commodity, your nimble opponent may have sold several smaller sets and be speeding the round towards its conclusion.  He may even have sold some goods of the type you are collecting, to reduce your set's value.  A fine game.

Production quality is very good and the artwork is quite appealing.  The rules are a clear and easy read.

RATING: 7
A game that is well designed for what it seeks to accomplish: a quick, interesting, two player card game.

Not exactly fireworks, and certainly not as magical (?!) in its appeal as the curiously pleasing mechanism of Coloretto, which would go on to evolve into the award winning boardgames of Zooleretto and Aquaretto, but worthy in its own way. The designer of Jaipur, Pauchon is truly a good game designer.  

You can play this game almost meditatively and feel like you "gamed" with a certain amount of intelligence and effort.  Yet, did you? Maybe so...

Because the online version handles the relatively involved set up and bookkeeping (at least for a card game) easily, curiously, this game perhaps plays better online.  In fact, this game could definitely be an international hit with the online gaming community.  Move over words with friends?  Well, not exactly, but there is clearly something "there" with this game.  I would call it almost meditative as an online experience.  On the other hand, I suppose one could agonize over all the decisions!  Nah, play it quick and meditatively! : )

Das Spiel ist neuen Mitspielern schnell erklärt. Da es sich schnell aufbauen lässt und sich auch schnell ein paar mal hintereinander spielen lässt, kommt es bei Wenigspielern bisher sehr gut an. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Initially I found this game to be quite enjoyable and of in the same vein as Battle line (stereotypical 2-player couple's game). But eventually I discovered that players could count the cards by simplify referencing the good tokens, which eliminated the whole memorization aspect; and that aspect was important as it allowed players to make informed decisions if they could track/memorize the cards that had been played.

But, after a couple of dozen plays... it dawned on me. There is little need to memorize what has been played because of the good tokens. Admittedly, I felt a bit stupid for not realizing it sooner. But it also reduced my enjoyment of the game because it made the luck/randomness element much more palatable. I started to see the outcomes of games being dictated by the luck of the draw... and I loss interest in playing it. 

Great components. Certainly a well-received game and something that many people find enjoyable. Just don't expect this game to withstand competitive play. 


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The components in this game are quite lovely, with the nice cards (quite easy to riffle shuffle) and lovely cardboard counters, with a thick enough. The insert to keep them all in is quite nice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Egy nagyon jól sikerült kétszemélyes kártyajáték! A tevék feldobják, pörgőssé teszik. Nagyon kiegyenlített, szinte mindig 80 pont körül szóródtunk, +-5 pont, egyszer volt 90 feletti pontszám (összesen 5 menet volt a 2 játék során).
---
14 játék után már ritkán nyerünk nagy különbséggel, ezért a pontszámok is kisebbek. Úgy gondolom, 70 pont feletti eredmény már nem rossz, ha egyenlő erők küzdenek. (Megjegyzés: ha jól számolom, az elméleti maximum 189 pont: 3 kivételével az összes áru = 127, teve = 5, az összes 5-ös bónuszlapka, és 2 db 4-es bónusz = 57... Persze ehhez az kell, hogy a másik fél 0 pontot érjen el, és a lapok megfelelő sorrendben jöjjenek.)
---
Kipróbáltuk 4 fővel, azért úgy nem az igazi:) De mégis megéri néha így is, más élményt ad!
---
Egyre több taktikai lehetőségre jövünk rá. Szerintem kisebb a szerepe a szerencsének, mint ahogy az elsőre látszik.
---
Majdnem 4 év telt el az utolsó beírásom óta:) Rengeteg partin vagyok túl, és aquir jóvoltából a neten is sikerült kipróbálnom. Nos elég lesújtó a véleményem a netes Jaipurról (és nem azért, mert aquir levert 3-2-re:)). Egyszerűen a Jaipur lényegét öli meg azzal a program, hogy mindent mutat: milyen lapok mentek ki, milyen lapokat vett fel ill. adott el az ellenfél, hány tevéje van, hány pontja(!) van, stb. Gyakorlatilag a kezdő lapjain kívül és a húzott bónuszain kívül minden ismert (még a bónuszokkal is lehet kalkulálni, mert pl. azt látjuk, hogy 67 és 75 pont között áll, tehát nagy eséllyel kb. 71-72 pontja lehet, és a saját pontunknál is oda van írva, hogy mondjuk 62 pontunk van, de az ellenfél annyit tud, hogy 58 és 65 között...) Az a gond, hogyha nem ezzel az esőember üzemmóddal játszanánk, akkor pedig előnybe kerülne az, aki írná, ha a másik esetleg nem írná. Márpedig ha naponta néhány lépés van csak, akkor muszáj lenne írni. Szóval pont az veszik így el, ami miatt szeretjük ezt a játékot: természetesen az élő játékban sosem vesszük annyira komolyan, hogy mindent memorizálni próbáljunk, rengetegszer múlik azon, hogy nem emlékszünk, hogy 10-15 körrel korábban vett-e fel az ellenfél mondjuk aranyat, és tök izgi bekockáztatni, hogy hátha nincs sárgája, vagy hogy hány tevéje lehet vajon, elég-e az 5 teve az 5 ponthoz. Vagy a legalapvetőbb kérdés, hogy ki vezet éppen? A gépen játszva az esetek 95%-ában pontosan tudhatom, hogy vezetek vagy sem. Élőben max ha külön számolom... Ezzel együtt, még így is, hogy számtanpéldává degradálódik a neten a játék, voltak hihetetlen izgalmas partik. Másfajta izgalom volt így, de még így is jó volt:) Lehet hogy kicsit túlértékelem ezt a játékot, de nekem továbbra is nagyon bejön.

RATING: 8.5
Loved it!  Simplicity breaking down two-player board gaming into it's component parts.  Thinking back on my first best-of-three play what comes to mind isn't the bits and cards and how we moved them around.  Instead, it was more an impression of how the flow of the game shifted back and forth between my opponent and I.  And how we acted and counter-acted, etc.  

I thought that spoke to the absolute simplicity of what's going on in the game.  I don't know if the "9" rating will stand the test of time though.  The reason being that I get the sense that the game could wear thin with further play, as if maybe it's too simple.  

So, if there's a weakness to this game, it might be that it's too simple and there isn't enough here.  I can live with that.  It's a '9' for now.  And I'd like to get a copy, when I can, just for those "one more game" nights or introducing friends to board games.

RATING: N/A
A++
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
It's a hit with my girlfriend, but also solid for strategy and tactics. That makes it a pretty big win in my book. Luck is not absent, but overall better play will win. And I'm always more forgiving of luck elements when a game plays quick and the winner is determined over multiple rounds rather than one. In this case it's whoever wins 2 rounds out of 3. (And I have to add, that the more we play the more often we find ourselves tied or 1 point away from each other's scores for the round! That's got to mean it's more balanced than it appears, right...?) You have to decide how much to push your luck, and it's necessary to monitor you opponent and see what cards he or she is grabbing, how many cards are in hand, how many camels are in his or her herd, etc. So tactically, you should be able to shift short term plans based on what your opponent is doing. Nicely interactive. Just a really solid 2 player game. The rules are extremely simple and leave room for surprisingly meaty choices. And most important, it's really fun!

RATING: 6
Straight-forward (and simple) rules and gameplay, but the game feels average.  Strategically, it's probably best to focus on one expensive set and one cheap set of goods (at any given time) in order to try to maximize your chances of getting good bonuses.  Now I just need to remember that you cannot sell solo gold, silver or red (diamond)!

UPDATE 2014: An okay but luck-heavy game of set collection and brinksmanship.  Sets consist of 2+ cards of the same type of good.  For most goods, the sooner you sell a set, the more points you'll get per individual card sold.  The hand limit of 7 cards is another major factor in deciding when/what to sell.  If you hold out longer and collect a set of 3+ cars, you'll earn bonus points based on the number of cards you sell.  The brinksmamship aspect of the game is primarily related to deciding when to take multiple camel cards since it can reveal several new cards (instead of one) which can be detrimental to your game if you reveal to many cards that help your opponent; therefore, it can be better to take just a single goods card (versus multiple camels).  That said, the game is won or lost by luck of the draw and carefully managing the hand limit.

RATING: 9
25 plays in: Has a calming flow to it, a rhythm and balance that make winning and losing not seem like the point. Setup is a pain in the ass, though, with sorting all the metal coin tokens by value, though once you get familiar with them, you don't really need to do more than sort by color. And "let's see who had the higher score" is always a weak way to end a game. Doesn't jump off the shelf like, say, Monopoly Deal, but we're always glad we played it.

The strategy is truly elegant and unique here -- the role of the camels is simple yet deepens the game immensely. And there is a direct back-and-forth push and pull of the players' impact on each other, which makes gameplay intimate and satisfying.

Gets asked for very often; always seems like fun and not homework, and yet skilled players definitely do better. Just a perfect gem of a game.

I do wish the front cover didn't have such a cringe-inducing caricature of an Indian man.

RATING: 7.5
This is a great two-player game. You can learn the rules in less than a minute, but the game is complex enough to keep your interest for the duration of the three rounds. 

The best aspect of the game is the fact that you have to draw from the market, and your cards are limited to the number of goods you have to trade. So you will sometimes have to give up cards you want, or give your partner cards he/she needs, in order to make the most lucrative trades.

RATING: 7
Rummy-type game with a card draft for draws and melds with values dependent on number of cards melded (higher value for more cards) and first to meld each suit (early melds with more than late melds).

Although this game is fun, the risk-reward element is not nearly as large as most people think because first melds and larger melds have the same value.

RATING: 6
Rated initially an "8." My wife tends to enjoy playing the same game numerous times instead of seeking something new. Consequently, if I guide her toward a game, I do so knowing that it better hold up over numerous plays (see: Ingenious, Lost Cities). 

Jaipur caught my interest as a sort of Lost Cities: Part II. And there's some definite similarities in how they play... quick rapid-fire turns, short game time, balance of luck and tactics, etc. At the same time, this offers enough "newness" that I'm prepared for the 50+ plays it will likely get!

Compared to Lost Cities, Jaipur has a more fiddly setup, with stacking all the coins in the correct order. But, the game itself offers some more choices and more direct interaction, which I'm thankful for. It's easier to try and "jam" your opponent with bad or limited choices (five camels?? Oh great.) Any fans of Lost Cities looking for a new "spouse game" should definitely check this out. 

Update:  Ultimately, this game sat on the shelf, and sat on the shelf... my wife and I simply couldn't get excited about this one. With 25+ plays, we had our fun with the game, but Jaipur has lost out to Battle Line and other two-player-friendly card game options.

RATING: 7.5

RATING: 8.5
Great girlfriend game. Two player, nice and simple, best of three rounds - so it doesn't hurt either side when you see that there is no way of winning: "Ill just try again next round!"

The rulebook is written VERY well. 

The chit system is very well put together and makes you click on to strategies after a few goes.

The camel cards are in a fine balance between being good and being bad in the wrong, or right hands. 

Very quick set up time if you pack it away correctly, and uses a "dont know your score till the end" mechanism which always makes games a little more exciting!

My girlfriend and I (now my finoce) actually went to Jaipur on holiday before buying this, made it all the more enjoyable 

Highly rated, cheap, small footprint, fun for 2 players

RATING: 10
An excellent, light, casual 2 player game. 

I love it for so many reasons. The first one being that 99% of the time, I'm playing board games with only one person (my boyfriend) and we're both pretty competitive people. This game is light enough (so neither of us are mad about losing) but still got that fun competitive edge. 

It plays really fast but you still have to be strategic. It's a good way to spend 15 minutes without feeling like you're mentally exhausted afterwards. Both players also draw from the same cards, so there are no uneven roles like a lot of 2 player games. It's a card game (with some extra tokens) so the box is very portable and good for travel.

PLUS: I'm a designer. There are a lot of games out there that are fun, but not really well designed IMO. This satisfies that design itch. The illustrations are bright and cute and the box looks great on my shelf.

Probably the most replayed and enjoyed game we have.

RATING: 7
I purchased this game during Spiel 2009 after two trial games; the first went very much awry because of the rather odd rules explanation which didn't seem to make any sense until I read through the rule book. Since then I've played about a dozen games, each time losing badly against my main gaming partner (the girlfriend), and ever since I figured out how to turn the tables a little, my appreciation for this card game has grown.

The idea is very simple, really. Open cards in various kinds (goods and camels) allow you to trade (meaning that you take two or more goods and replace them by cards in your hand and/or previously gathered camels), purchase (meaning taking one good and replacing it with a card drawn from the pile) or simply to get all camels, (which are then all replaced by cards taken from the draw pile). At some point you want to be collecting sets and sell them, for which you get a equal number of chips with points on them and, if you sold enough in one go, a bonus chip. The trick is that the value of the chips diminishes so that selling the same goods later in the game won't net you as many points as in the beginning.

As I said: simple, and you'd be absolutely right. But to play it well is [i]not[/i] simple, despite what you would be able to read in the negative comments. The main problem is that any action which fills the open spaces of taken-up cards by cards from the draw pile might give your opponent a very valuable set which he then sells on his own. But at the same time you cannot keep on selling forever because the net result is too meager as the chips are too low in value. You need to obtain sets in that case, and that means hoarding cards and biding your time. These and other simple dynamics create an atmosphere where both players must watch each other like hawks, and be forever on the lookout to score lots of points as efficiently as possible, to the point where card counting becomes a necessity of sorts. The bonus chips introduce a little uncertainty in the entire procedure to prevent complete control, which keeps things fresh and interesting without undue worries—although theoretically the game can be lost on the randomised point difference. More worrying is that in some cases a player getting a very good selection of extremely valuable goods out of sheer luck of the draw more or less walks away with victory right there and then. In a game where timing, hand management and a bit of memory are key components, such  occurrences feel like jamming the proverbial wrench in the gears. True, the game is fast enough to demand a rematch, but still. 

Despite this tiny rough edge I found myself liking this game quite a lot, and would heartily recommend it over that ageing Lost Cities. Jaipur scratches the same itch, but deciding what to do when is a more involved, making it more of an interesting experience to see if your luck paid off. I'm quite interested to see whether I will hit a point beyond which I will not play this game any longer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A great trading game with a great theme! Beautiful artwork that you get to hold and trade, nice components, easy to learn and teach, fast setup and play time, engaging and exciting with equal parts chance and decisiveness, this game offers a lot in a little box.

I really enjoy the various aspects of this game that demand balance from the player, including:
1. A hand limit that forces trading and tradeoffs between cards held and those desired in the market;
2. The ability to take any one card from the market or exchange any number of cards, meaning a player can increase his holdings slowly by direct acquisition or pay the price of stagnating to quickly obtain several sought after cards at once through even exchange - such that opportunity cost functions get involved in this market simulation; and
3. A tiered point system that rewards timely action: will you trade your few goods in for the quick early bird advantage of a high payout, or risk the odds and hold out for a potentially high-value set?

Add to all this the thrill of revealing unknown new cards every turn, one of which could be your coveted prize, and you have an addictive game for any night of the week, and you only need one other person to play!

I bought this game to play with a friend who doesn't enjoy as much our gaming group's heavier Eurogames, but still likes a challenge and a good theme - and it foots the bill perfectly. Will play again and again.

RATING: 7.25
This is a fast and light-hearted game for two players.  Through trading or acquiring cards in the market, you try to collect sets and then trade them in for the bonus tiles of that color when you sell.  The twist is you really need to manipulate caravans (camel cards) to acquire several cards at once.

I like the choices facing players - to cash in on the valuable items, or go deep with the cheap ones?  Should you strive to be the caravan master for a few bonus points, or use those cards quickly to get a nice haul?

Supremely easy to learn and very fast to play.  This is a good filler while watching a TV show or waiting for dinner to finish cooking.  Bright and colorful, this also would do well with kids.

RATING: 8
I get the feeling that Jaipur is flying a little below the radar on BGG.  The reviews I've seen recommended Jaipur as the "next good spouse friendly" two player game.  I have to agree.  My wife readily plays, and has been the one to suggest a second or third play.  After a few games, I'm beginning to think Jaipur reminds me of Ticket to Ride more than anything else.  Sure, there is no board, but the basic mechanism is set collection to purchase a limited supply of points.  The limited hand size, the camel trading, the varying point values, and the bonuses, all lead to a surprising amount of tactical decisions.

RATING: 7.5
I'm really not sure about this game. I've played it several times, and I'm not sold on it. It just feels like all you do is collect cards then turn them in--not exactly exciting! It could be that we haven't always been very careful to ensure we don't have more than seven cards in hand. Some of the comparisons I've seen of Jaipur to Lost Cities are absolutely ridiculous! There's no comparison whatsoever!

Update: I've made up my mind: I do like this game. We used to play this without paying much attention to the hand-limit (we forgot about it). If you play strictly with the seven card hand-limit, the game really shines, offering tough decisions. It's a great 2-player game, and I'd much rather play this over Lost Cities.

RATING: 8
Jaipur was a really positive surprise for me. The rules are very simple and yet the gameplay offers plenty space for strategic and tactical play. The way this two player game simulates trading is interesting - you are in a way making offers to the other player, trying to get him to react in the way that benefits you. There is a slight amount of randomness, which is a good thing because it seriously limits the possibility of analysis paralysis this game might otherwise cause.

RATING: 7.5
Rating based on initial impressions...

Bought this game specifically to play with my wife after reading what has been posted here and from what I can tell so far, I will enjoy this game.

But my wife had a pretty negative reaction to it, which surprised me. She didn't like that an action or possible mistake on her part led to an immediate negative consequence. She might have simply been unlucky, but I did always seem to get the diamond/gold cards ahead of her. It's too soon to tell if she made poor decisions or if she truly kept having bad luck. She had this to say: "I feel like the game is playing me and not the other way around." She likes games where she can utilize long-term planning and she never felt she could take such an approach with this one. Given how much she liked Archaeology: The Card Game, I certainly didn't expect such a reaction from her.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players are commodity dealers, buying and selling for their Maharajah.  Lightning quick two player melding game, where players are either drawing/exchanging cards with a market pool on the table, or cashing in melds for different sets of victory points.  The treasures require more melded cards but are worth more points.  Round ends when three sets are cleaned out, and total up your points.  Bonus points awarded for 3/4/5 card melds and collecting camel cards.  Play best of three rounds.  Easy to teach, probably plays out in 20 minutes.  Very enjoyable two player filler.

RATING: 6.5
This is a fun little game but it didn't blow me away.  At first it seemed like there wasn't enough tension, but the tension grew in the second round when we paid closer attention to which point tokens were remaining and what they were worth.  One thing I appreciated was how easy the game was to learn.  It plays quickly as well.  I liked it, but I'm having trouble finding specific things that I liked about it.

Update: After a second playing, it still feels like something is missing, and I've lowered the rating. The decision making is quite simple, there isn't a lot of strategy, and there is a fair amount of luck influencing the outcome. It's not a bad game, and it's cute, but it's just not interesting enough.  There are too many other 2 player games that we like to play, so I've put this up for trade.

RATING: 9
This is a fun card game for 2 players. Basically you take turns going back and forth either buying or selling (but never both) each turn until one player amasses enough points to win the round, and the winner of the game is best 2 out of three rounds. I really like the card art, and I like how the game plays, not too complicated, but with enough strategy to keep it interesting. The big thing about this game is the decision each round to buy or sell. At first, its pretty obvious, you want to buy to get some good cards, BUT, the first person to buy into each set of goods gets most of the high point markers, so just waiting one turn could cost you a lot of points. Each turn you don't buy is another turn your opponent can buy and take a huge lead, but at the same time, each turn you buy means another couple of turns building your hand back up so that you can buy again. Fun game, I don't think its one you master right off the bat.

RATING: 9
Cards with six different gems are traded in for stacks of VP tokens, each with a different distribution of values. Everything revolves around how cards are picked up and managed in hand. Choosing the right time to trade, or forcing opponents to trade really matters. Camel cards add an interesting hold mechanic, refusing to reveal new cards from the deck for opponents to snatch up. A lot of tense ebb and flow to hand management between experienced players.

15-30m, 2 players. 30+ plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Jaipur is a 2-player game of trading, tempo, and set collection. You don't trade with your opponent. Rather, you trade with a row of cards accessible to both players. Cards come in 2 varieties: goods and camels. Camels are useful because they don't clog up your hand limit of cards and a majority of them at the end of the round provides a scoring bonus. On your turn you can either sell goods, or acquire cards. When you sell cards, you can only sell one type of good, but as many of that type as you want (though half of the good types require a minimum of 2 goods). The game has incentives to sell early (a good's value decrease as goods sell) and to wait (selling more cards at once provide you bonus chips with is worth additional points). When acquiring cards, you can either take 1 good, all camels, or trade goods/camels you own for goods in the center. The danger of taking cards (particularly multiple camels) is that you could be giving the opponent the cards they need. You play the best 2 of 3 rounds. It's quick, easy to explain, but has interesting decisions and unique gameplay. Definitely a good lighter game recommended for couples.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Best two player card game by far! Cards and components are beautiful and the game itself is very fun. The market system is clean and the ebbs-and-flows vary from game to game. Luck is present, but the camels are a surprisingly useful luck mitigation tool, and create wonderful decisions throughout the game. Should you pick up the 4 Camel cards, and allow yourself great flexibility later in the game, or should you leave them in the market and allow your opponent to open up the market for you? In a similar way, the timing of selling your cards is crucial, as an small early sale of leather, can easily trump a larger one later in the game. A must buy for any two-player fan.

RATING: 5
Jaipur is a harmless, fast playing game for two. It's your classic 'on your turn you can do this or that, but not both' game. The set collection aspect is simple and effective. Overall I think it's a likable title, but there's nothing there that really endears me to the game. The set up is a little more tedious than the average cards-only 2p game, but only by a little. If you like Lost Cities and you value card art and general game display, this is a title that may interest you.   

I ended up trading this title away. In the end, I did find the game interesting, and probably better than most 2p games, BUT the setup was a real dealbreaker.

RATING: 8
After having played Jaipur 60+ times, I think the sheen of it has worn off a little bit. I'd rank it a 9 for my first fifty games, but by now the motions have become a little bit repetitive -- hence my rating of 8. Don't let that put you off from trying this wonderful game, though! Jaipur is a fantastic two-player game with quite a bit of strategy and a dose of luck. It's more fun than Lost Cities and has more options for winning. There's a fair amount of screwage, and the semi-random bonus tiles add a needed amount of uncertainty to the scoring. Jaipur easily plays in half an hour, and it doesn't outstay its welcome. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Jaipur is a game that forces you, as many tight two-player games do, to weigh each decision you make. When to collect, when to trade in, when to break down and take all the camels.... I find it a greater challenge to choose than I expected.

The game conceals the depth of its decision-making, for the actions themselves are few and simple.

I've found, after many plays, that scores are usually tight. This is not a game of one-sided blowouts.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a clever 2P card game that has some very nice considerations that keep it from feeling zero sum. It's a set collection race where, on your turn, you can either take cards or turn them in for VPs. You gotta make sure you turn in your cards before the round ends else they don't count. 

The thrust of the game is the way you may replace cards you take with cards you have. Some of the cards you have may be camel cards that don't contribute to the sets you're trying to make but simply serve as replacements. Ha, take that, opponent! They do at least, however, confer a 5VP bonus to whomever ends up with the most at the end of the round.

Sometimes you may go for the higher value sets or you may go for a bunch of lower value ones and the camels. What you take and what you leave for your opponent are both important considerations. 

Clever game. You play best of 3 rounds.

RATING: 4.5
Jaipur is a card game representing a contest between two traders to see who can get richer.

[u]Overview[/u]
Players start with five randomly dealt cards. Camels always go into the herd and goods form a player's hand (maximum size: 7 goods). Five cards are placed on the board, forming the market; all the other cards form the deck. Every turn a player can draw all the camels on the market (replacing them with new cards from the deck), exchange cards with the market (taking 2+ goods and replacing them with cards not of the same kinds), draw a single good from the market (replacing it with a new card from the deck) or sell goods of a single kind for rupees. If a group of three or more goods is sold at once, the seller gets a bonus. The goods' value is represented by the good tokens and decreases as more of them are sold. When there are no tokens left for three different goods, or when the market cannot be refilled because the deck is empty, the round ends. The player who has more rupees from sales and bonuses wins the round. The first player to win two rounds wins the game.

[u]Strategy and comments[/u]
The rules are simple and promising, but towards the end most close rounds tend to grind to a halt, because nobody wants to risk exposing cards that would benefit their opponent. This means both players will try to burn as many turns as possible while doing almost nothing in order to force their opponent to reveal a new card which, if they're lucky or unless they're unlucky, will let them score big (or block their opponent) and win the round.

This is caused by too much information being available: you know exactly (from the tokens) what has been sold, you know what your opponent has drawn recently and everything else can only be in the deck. When the deck gets thin you know how likely it is for you to reveal a bad card, so you start wasting turns to avoid doing that. This could have been solved by adding one more card for each good and removing some of them randomly at the beginning of every round.

Hoarding goods of a kind to sell them for a higher bonus is better after the goods' value drops; camels grant a lot of flexibility and shouldn't be ignored; exchanging cards with the market is improved by having camels and is a nice way to get goods you want without revealing new cards; timing is key: for example, groups of camels on the market should be taken when the opponent is unable to profit from the goods that will be revealed (usually because of the hand limit or of having no camels); often at the beginning of the game it is worth to sell just one or two cards of a good and losing out on the bonus if you know your opponent is hoarding them, in order to drive down the price.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
If you're looking for inspiration on how to achieve a lot with very little, Jaipur is a very good example. With only a stack of cards, a couple of bonus chits and a set of rules that you could almost fit on a coaster there is still a very compelling game in the box.

Players are trading sets of goods cards for bonus chits, and always need to weigh immediate benefits against possibly higher benefits later on - provided the other player doesn't cash in first and grabs most of the high value chits which would handily ruin those nice bonus profits.

It's short, it's light, simply a very good game for two all around.

RATING: 6
Jaipur er et solidt to-spillers spil, der har fået en positiv modtagelse og som også falder fint i min smag. Det er et oplagt spil i kategorien kærestespil for selvom der er handel og det går ud på at sikre sig de bedste varer og hurtigst muligt få lavet nogle lukrative handler inden priserne daler, så er det så godt justeret, at de spil vil har spillet er endt overraskende tæt. I spillet erhverver man sig forskellige varer, hvor nogle er mere lukrative end andre, priserne daler efterhånden som der er blevet solgt varer af en bestemt type og man belønnes for at sælge store mængder af samme type vare. Man kan i sin tur vælge enten at sælge eller at købe varer. Køber man varer har man lov til enten at tage en vare blandt dem, der ligger fremme på bordet eller at bytte så mange man vil. Da man samler varer sammen på hånden og samtidig har en øvre grænse for hvor mange varer man må have på hånden, er det nødvendigt ind imellem at få solgt ud af de varer man har på hånden. Hvis man sidder med en hel hånd af kort, man har samlet, kan modstanderen med en smule held og eller omtanke sørge for at store dele af markedet bliver udskiftet uden at man har mulighed for at erhverve sig disse, da det ville betyde, at man måtte skaffe sig af med de varer, man slidsomt har skrabet sammen. Så timing betyder meget i spillet. Udover varer er der yderligere en korttype: Kameler. Kameler lægger man i en bunke foran sig på bordet. Den fungerer som en udvidet hånd, der ikke tæller med, når man tæller op, hvor mange kort man har på hånden. Kamelerne kan man dog også anvende til at bytte for varer, så selvom man lige har solgt alle de varer man havde på hånden, betyder det ikke nødvendigvis, at man ikke kan foretage store indkøb på markedet. Afslutningsvis er der en bonus for at have flest kameler i sin bunke. Bunken med kameler er i mine øjne det twist, der før Jaipur interessant. Et par ting gør dog, at det næppe kommer særlig ofte på bordet hos os; dels er jeg nok den, der syntes bedst om spillet og det begrænser jo brugen, hvis det skal anvendes som kærestespil og dels har vi mange andre to-spillers spil som jeg trods alt også finder er langt mere interessante end Jaipur. Men det betyder langt fra, at jeg ikke vil anbefale spillet. En lille detalje der irriterer mig ved spillet er, at reglerne foreskriver, at man skal spille spille indtil en spiller har vundet to spil uden at score dog overføres fra spil til spil. Det behøver man selvfølgelig ikke gøre, men i mine øjne er det en unødvendig forskrift på hvordan spillet skal spilles, når nu der i øvrigt ikke overføres nogen form for status fra det ene spil til det næste.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
After playing Jaipur, I realized why it's on the list of everyone's top 10 for 2 player game.

Even though there is a slight luck involved in the game, the game does have a tiny amount of strategy in terms of selecting cards. The camel cards and how it plays is really nice, where you have to take all the camels or none. It might seem like an advantage in the beginning but having a lot of camel herd can be anti-climatic for certain scenarios.

It's very simple, easy to teach, well made and I think a peaceful game :)

If you want to introduce your spouse to board-gaming slowly, dose-by-dose and ramp up the complexity, then Jaipur is a good start for that. And even then, down the line, you will still take it out to play. 

Really like the game.

RATING: 7
Nice little 2-player game. It takes about 15 minutes to play, though the rules tell you to play it 2-3 times, so figure about 30 to 45 minutes. In a nutshell, every turn you draw cards to make sets of 2,3,4,or 5 of a kind, or you sell those sets to get victory points. You can also get a +5 bonus if you have the most camels. That's pretty much it. It's a nice game with broad appeal, nice components, and generally recommended.

RATING: 8
A great two-player trading game. Each player collects sets of cards representing different goods. Depending how large your set is, you can get bonus points. Unfortunately, there are a limited number of each good, and their value decreases the longer you wait. So, do you take few goods now for higher value, or risk gathering a larger set for less points but a larger bonus? Excellent give and take decisions in a relatively short amount of time.

RATING: 5
Jaipur is a quick playing push your luck set collection game. Players collect sets of each color and must choose either to build a larger set for larger profit or sell early to ensure collection of the limited available points tokens. On the downside, setup has a few too many pieces for the type of game. Each resource and bonus has a stack and each stack needs to be stacked sequentially. It is small, but for the speed of the game, setup time stands out as too long. As for strategy, interesting decisions boil down to whether take camel control and risk newly revealed cards benefiting your opponent or take one card and limit that risk. Rummy style two player card games appear quite often. Among those, Jaipur is good, but doesn't have the greatness of others. What hold it back the most from seeing several plays is the somewhat tedious setup. As of now, the electronic implementation of Jaipur is lovely, but on the table, less so.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

An awesome quick playing two players game. There's more strategy than it seems at first sight. Love it for the price.

RATING: 7
Very good two player filler game.  Im thinking the replayability is zero but for what it is, a super light filler you can play with anyone.  Most hands play themselves but you do get a few good push your luck decisions and when you decide to take camels can be vital to your success.  BUT a decision-heavy game it isn't, even during your first game the decisions will seem very very obvious.

 A good "intro" to gaming game, or something you can drag off the shelf when a friend visits and says "what are all these games" because it teaches in two minutes, plays quick, etc.  

I like it for what it is - if you go in with the wrong expectations you will not like it.

RATING: 6.75
Bontott, egyszer játszott
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Gran juego para dos jugadores. Las partidas son rápidas y divertidas. Desde luego, hay un factor de azar, pero, a menos que los planetas se hayan alineado en tu contra, no es decisivo: gana quien mejor juega sus cartas.

A grandes rasgos, el juego consiste en hacerse con mercancías. No todas son igual de valiosas e influye la cantidad que acumulas: vender 3, 4 ó 5 proporciona valiosos bonus. Pero la clave está en forzar al contrario a robar todas las cartas de la mesa, lo que aumenta las posibilidades propias de obtener las mejores mercancías.

En definitiva, un buen juego para dos, con reglas sencillas, fácil preparación, agradable de jugar y reñido cuando se adquiere cierta experiencia.

RATING: 8
This is a really cool little two-player card game.  It's a rummy variant insofar as players are trying to make sets and need to discard to a shared area where their opponent can pick up the cards they put down, but unlike, say, Lost Cities, it has a distinctive flavor of its own.  I enjoyed the brinksmanship of trying to save up for a token that your opponent may also be saving for. The camels are also a neat touch; they can serve as a wild card, but having more than your opponent is also worth points. I think the Spiel has it right when they say this feels like a traditional card game without feeling like a traditional card game that already existed.  (1 play)

RATING: 9
Jaipur is an outstanding game that makes a trading theme for 2 players super fun. You have to play the market (i.e. take cards from the spread) to collect sets and trade them in for chips for those goods. The more and earlier you trade, the better return you get for those goods. There’s some tactics, a little risk and pushing your luck, and it’s so fun trying to beat your opponent to the better rewards! I guarantee you’ll be playing back to back games of Jaipur.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.26
La idea del juego es muy sencilla y está ambientada en un mercado de la ciudad oriental de Jaipur. Hay una serie de cartas disponibles en la mesa que representan mercancías y camellos. Puedes comerciar (tomar dos o más cartas y reemplazarlas por las que tuvieras en tu mano y/o por camellos que tuvieras en tu poder), comprarlas (tomas una mercancía y la reemplazas por otra carta del mazo de robo) o sencillamente, tomas todos los camellos que haya en la muestra (y dejas en su lugar cartas extraídas del mazo de robo). A la vez, tienes que tratar de cumplir el objetivo del juego, que consiste en hacer "familias" (sets) de cartas de la misma mercancía y venderlos para conseguir tantos puntos como mercancías vendas,(una ficha con forma de moneda) y, si vendes los suficientes (3 o más), un bonus por haberlo hecho. La gracia está en que el valor de las monedas que tomas, va decreciendo y además, no hay para los dos jugadores, así que vender las mismas mercancías conforme avanza el juego, te reporta cada vez menos puntos.

PROS: El límite de 7 cartas en la mano, los bonus por vender 3 o + mercancías, los 5 PV´s al que más camellos tenga... le añaden mucha más tensión de la que podría esperarse en un juego a 2.

CONS: Quizá demasiado simple. Dosis de azar.

Un buen juego para 2 que es una mezcla de Euro y juego de cartas tradicional.

RATING: 9
A stunning 2 player-only game in which you are trading different commodities for points.  The game plays very simply but contains a surprising amount of strategy - a large part of which stems from the use of camel cards, which you cannot sell but only trade in return for other available cards.  The fact you have a hand limit, along with a few other factors, make this a very interesting and highly competitive game.

The game looks beautiful, comes in a small box, and is perfect for playing with your SO.  Highly recommended.

RATING: 8.3
I've introduced this to a lot of people and they all enjoyed it. Very good 2p game, maybe my favorite.  Lots of potential for strategy, but simple to learn. Some luck involved, but not too much. my rating keeps creeping up

competitive but not adversarial. The decisions you have made give your an opponent an advantage or disadvantage but you KNOW ahead of time what risk you're taking. There isn't any real "take that" about it, so it doesn't feel mean at all. It DOES have brutally tough decisions despite being a nice quick game that is easy to learn. Also, it's cheap.  Definitely my go-to 2 player game.

RATING: 9
An excellent compact trading game for two players. It is surprisingly engaging for a relatively simple card game, whilst still fast playing. Each turn counts, with players needing to optimise their collection and trading of leather, silk and other wares. Adapting strategy to the turn of the cards and decisions of the other player makes for an enjoyable challenge. Elegant, ‘thinky-but-light’, portable and aesthetically pleasing – a real benchmark for small-box games.

Initial Rating: 7.0 (August 2012)

Initially I really liked this game, nowadays I have cooled a little toward it. This is a good family game, with simple rules but with some interesting/hard decisions, still it is a bit light for my taste. The game flows rather quickly and my GF loves it so I don’t think I will sell it. The theme is paper thin for me.

I’m downgrading its rating to a 6.0 so it matches the BGG recommendation system with my new feelings for the game. 

Jaipur is great to initiate new gamers to our hobby or to play with your family. 

Current Rating: 6.0
[hr]
2014-03-10

After lots of more plays, I’m downgrading Jaipur´s rating again. It just has a lot of uncontrollable luck in it and now it bothers me a lot, It has some interesting decisions, but luck decides the winner 80% of the time. The only reason I don’t sell it is because my GF loves the game and request it often. Gorgeous components though. 

Current Rating: 5.0
[hr]
2015-08-06

I sold Jaipur. If my GF wants to play it we will do it online.

RATING: N/A
[b][url=url=http://www.spieldesjahres.de/cms/front_content.php?idcat=8]2010 Spiel de Jahres Recommended[/url][/b]

My kingdom for a camel. Work hard, earn more than your opponent and become the official merchant of the Maharaja. At the beginning of the game, three camel cards and two merchandise cards are on the table between the players, who already have five cards in hand. On your turn, you can take some cards or sell some. Each time that you take cards, you can take one or more, however if you decide to take more, then you will have to trade in the same amount of cards from your hand that you take. If you decide to take all camel cards, then they can be used later to trade. You can sell all the merchandise that you want, as long as they are all the same type. For each sale, you will receive tokens with various point values. At the end of the game, the player who has the majority of the camels also wins points.

Players: 2
Time: 30 min
Mechanics: [property=2040][/property], [property=2004][/property], [property=2008][/property]

RATING: 7
One of the better small-box two-player games I've played. It plays quick and you have judgement calls to make every turn. The game is almost entirely tactical since you won't know really what you'll have to work with on your turn, but staying flexible is the key to enjoying this game.

UPDATE 8/15: I've since acquired the game and started playing it with my 8-year-old daughter, and we can have competitive games. So I'd say the 12+ age on the box is incorrect. I think the reason for this is that every turn you take an action that helps you. There are no bad choices, just good, better, and best. Make more best choices than good choices and you'll win. My daughter was at first too enamoured with having a big herd, but she's quickly seeing that a herd is there to be used, not hoarded!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Great two-player set collection game and hand management economic filler that plays in about 15 minutes.  

The economic aspect of this game forces you to make some risk/reward trade offs.  Do you get your goods to market to get the higher price, or do you wait to get a larger collection and receive a market receive bonus?  Do you grab the herd of camels that can help you later, but may provide your opponent the goods they need now?  

My wife enjoys this one which is always a plus. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
About as light as a 2P cardgame can be without being a waste of time. Jaipur is perfectly fine as a wind-down game or a short break. Two players are drawing cards from an available row of 5 and then cashing them in. The game is all about controlling the card row. You want your opponent's choices to be as poor as possible.

RATING: 7
It's probably the odd colour mix that kept me from Jaipur so far which would have been a pity as I would have missed a really good two-player game. Naturally, it has to bear the comparison with Jambo as another two-player game of becoming the richest trader. While Jambo is the deeper game and leaves more to explore, Jaipur scores with ease of gameplay while still leaving plenty of decisions. Players have to weigh the benefit of scoring now for safe points or later for more risky higher points that in the worst case might not materialize at all. Recommended and I might seek this out for my collection.

RATING: 7
A nice little game with attractive, high quality components. It is quick to pick up and play.

There is a fair bit of luck in the fall of the cards, however there are interesting dynamics in how you can try to force the market against your opponent.

This is done through the manipulation of the camel herds, or use of low value cards, to acquire better cards in the market place -- you run the risk that by sweeping the market, an even better set of cards will be revealed from the draw pile.

There is also an important strategy in how you control the end of the game, which happens either when three commodities are exhausted, or the draw pile runs out. Selling your collected commodities is a turn, so you have to make sure the game lasts long enough to sell all you have in hand.

Overall it is not an awesome game, but it is a nice game and plays quickly enough that the lack of serious depth is not to its detriment.

RATING: N/A
Jaipur, capital of Rajasthan. You are one of the two most powerful traders in the city.

But that's not enough for you, because only the merchant with two Seals of Excellence will have the privilege of being invited to the Maharaja's court.

You are therefore going to have to do better than your direct competitor by buying, exchanging and selling at better prices, all while keeping an eye on both your camel herds.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There's a lot more to Jaipur than players often credit, especially those who've only played against one or two players, and not seen what 'higher level' play can involve.

Jaipur is a game of card counting, (mis)representation, timing, and positioning. Understanding the implications between the mechanics of camels, trading, and other cards is the unique element that makes the game interesting.

Knowing what your opponent is looking for or has is one of the primary factors in consideration as to camel and trade timing, and successfully bluffing in these moments can be decisive. Knowing when to sell un-optimally order to cut your opponent and gain the better draw position is as tricky to determine as knowing when to stop looking for a card you were chasing in order to switch out for a 5 card opportunity.

Knowing how to play an opponent with 1 card in hard and no camels is easy, but knowing how to play one with 1 card in hard and three camels it more interesting, and knowing how to play when they have 7 cards is also interesting.

And, despite all the above, the game has significant variance, enabling any player to win on their day, and often win at least one of three rounds in a loss, making it more fun and enjoyable for all players, not just the one that is better at it.

RATING: 7
Due to all of the rave reviews, I got a copy of [thing=54043][/thing] for my wife's birthday.  It's positioned as one of those light card games that are great for two players.  In this case, both players are trying to collect sets of different goods before trading them in for money.  In this case, there's a common pool of cards that you have to trade with.  This gives the game a bit of subtle player interaction since you have to be careful what you leave behind in the common pool.  The camels are a unique resource that act almost like a safety valve.  There's also a sense of trying to cash in goods before the other player, but you're also rewarded with waiting by getting bonuses for three or more goods that you trade in at the same time (this mechanism felt a little like [thing=21790][/thing] to us).

We found it to be an okay game, almost slightly over-hyped here.  The interaction is subtle, although generally the right choice feels obvious.  Often, the gameplay feels more like avoiding opening up new cards for your opponent rather than taking cards for yourself.  The shortness of the game is defeated by the relatively long setup time---the chips are quite fiddly to organize and arrange.  I don't see this being as portable as other card games of similar weight.  For this reason alone, it probably won't supplant [thing=50][/thing] for us, but it also lacks the tension of [thing=50][/thing] as well.  Compared to [thing=31105][/thing], another set collection game with a common pool, [thing=54043][/thing] feels a little more mechanical and less fun.  Not a bad two player game, but probably not our first choice off the shelf.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Very non-intuitive, the strategy.  But the game is simple enough that I can wrap my head around it.  

It pairs with the theme fairly well.  Very well when you consider how small of a game this is.

Mostly strategy, but a fair amount of luck in what cards come out when.  So far that doesn't bother me though.

Love the small footprint of the box.

Love that it's a 2-player game that has a fair amount of strategy, feels balanced, and a fair amount of theme.  Fills a very unique niche and I love that.

Components are good but certainly not stellar.

Mechanics are very simple while not limiting depth.  That's a great combo.

The only downside is that it feels hard to know how to get ahead.  But there are strategies to learn, they just aren't necessarily intuitive.  Like when to get camels--not as obvious as you think.  It depends on how full your opponent's hand is.

Normally when player interaction is just done through "if I buy this you can't" I'm not a fan.  In this case it's also "if I sell this you can't."  And in this, buying things so someone else can't is like the whole game, so the fact that this is the focus and drive makes the game very enjoyable to me actually.  I get a very particular feeling from playing this game and I like that.

RATING: 7
 I was interested in getting was a short 2 player game for a filler or just a light quick game before bed time. We got Parade last year and it filled it admirably, but some variety was desired. This (along with Biblios) is one of the most recommended games I've seen for that type of situation, so I picked it up. My pre-purchase unfounded prejudice this time was that it would, as a 2 player trading game, be too much like Jambo, which was utterly wrong. While Jambo always feels constrained - in a how do I get all the goods I need for this sale together in my limited space way - Jaipur feels very airy and open: buy or sell, pick one, swap a few. Turns are quick and we are certainly playing quite loosely. Is there a deeper strategy? Perhaps. Making use of camels and countering your opponents moves are certainly things we could improve upon. Another that looks like it is perfect for its purpose.

RATING: 9
Attractive components, and easy-to-learn rules; I foresee this replacing Lost Cities as the default recommendation for people to lure their non-gaming partners into the hobby. I thoroughly enjoy this game, but I must say that I do begin to wonder whether it's skill, or luck, that plays a bigger part in the outcome; in our games, my wife and I are tied for wins/losses, and so far, every game has gone for three rounds (the game uses best-of-three to determine the winner). Nevertheless, if it happens that luck is a huge factor, that's okay, because it certainly doesn't feel that way when you're playing.

Update: I think I was right about this replacing Lost Cities. This still sees a lot of play in our house.

RATING: 8
Excellent card game.  Easy to learn, the cards have clear iconography and are beautiful to look at.  There is nothing that distracts from the essence of the game.  No strange or arcane rules to remember, only a simple and elegant mechanism.  There is an element of luck that is more like excitement.  In other words, although the game surprises and and has an element of push your luck, it still feels like you are playing the game (instead of the game playing you) and that you have many possible and viable strategies and choices you can make regardless of the luck of the draw.  The give and take, ebb and flow of cards, is original enough and very interesting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A great 2-player game. One of my favorite parts of games is when the metagame starts to develop. Some games it takes a long time for the metagame to start to develop. You spend most of the time you play the game just getting used to it, that you don't have to time think about what could happen, or what your opponents might do if you do so and so. In Jaipur, the barrier to playing is so low that the metagame starts to develop in just your first play through. Every choice seems to obvious and the game starts to seem like it's boring, but then the metagame kicks in. You start wondering if picking up this obvious pile of camels is exactly what your opponent wants. It's a great game, that every couple should play through at least a couple of times.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is the best 2 player game I have ever played. The components are beautiful - artwork and quality are outstanding. The game plays true to theme, but not a slave to it. Instead you get a tightly designed game with excellent production value.

The core mechanic of trading cards with the market and swapping them in sets for chips is nice enough, but the addition of the camels makes this game shine. The camels introduce a timing element - when do I sell? When do I take the camels? How is my opponent's hand; if it's full I will do something markedly different than if it's empty.

Overall, the perfect blend of luck and strategy.

RATING: 9

Lots to like not the least of which is that goods tokens have points on the back making tallying points at the end a breeze... 4/17 -- Addendum
After multiple plays on BoardGameArena, my ranking of this game has waned slightly.  While there is strategy in when to grab goods, when to sell goods, and when to pick up camels, the luck involved can feel more variable than I'd like.  However, the game is particularly accessible and easily played within a short time frame; and therefore, will not be leaving my collection soon.  Solid 8 which I doubt will devolve further.

RATING: 9
The perfect definition for a filler. Games go for 15-25 minutes maximum.

Jaipur is a set collection game and the rules can be learned in under 10 minutes as each player only has 1 of 2 options per turn and only another couple of options under the taking cards option. 

The game is really all about hand management as the 7 card hand limit is very important. The game balances risk versus reward by offering up tokens of diminishing value. So you can go after them quickly but doing so means you won't build up a set in that good type to secure the 3, 4 or 5 card bonus scoring tiles.

It all works really well and lives up to the strong rep that I had heard about. 

A great 2-player game that my partner loves. The luck factor is about 60/40 but in a game such as this I have no problem with that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I don't quite understand the love this gets on BGG.  Something like gin rummy or farkel is just as interesting a game for two, in my opinion.  Jaipur is a quick set collection game with clean but slightly arbitrary mechanics.  Not bad, not fascinating. Just something to pass the time w/o thinking too hard.  

In Jaipur you've got a "market" of 5 cards. On most turns, a player will take 1 card from the market and replace it with a card from the deck.  Occasionally (but rarely), you'll take more than one card from the market, and here you've got to replace them from your own hand.  You cash in by playing cards of a set, and for each card you get a VP-coin of that set type...and these coins increase slightly in value the more that have been acquired previously.  There are a limited number of coins in each set, and when 3 types run out, the game / round is over.  Oh, and there is a slight incentive to hold out for larger set collections in the form of a random VP for sets of 3, 4 and 5.

That's basically it.  I'm leaving a few things out, but not much.  The game is a put together really well in terms of card distributions for each set, VP increments for each set, and VP incentives for accumulating larger sets.  It is also quick to learn.  I do find the internal consistency somehow lacking, though.  Lost Cities is a very similar game, but somehow its rules add up.  In Jaipur the rules seem just a bit arbitrary to me.  Well thought out, and yet arbitrary.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
In this 2 player game in Jaipur, you are trading goods. On your turn you either take cards to form a set or you sell the sets in your hand. The more cards you sell, the more of a bonus you will get. The round ends when the deck runs out or 3 types of goods have been depleted. You play best 2 out of 3 rounds. 

It's a quick playing game and fun. There is a luck element, when you take a card, and the next card up could be a rare card for your opponent to take. That's probably the only part I dislike about this game, but it is fun.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Light but perky filler boasting trades and nothing but trades: starting with similar hands, two players must use their respective turns either to draft one of several "jewel" cards face up on the table, exchange several of these for cards in their hand or camels, sweep all camels available on the table... or sell sets of jewel cards in one single colour for points. The sooner jewels are exchanged, the higher the score. The more jewels are exchanged, the higher the bonus.

As frequent as the topic and the mechanics are, it's quite a surprise that there hadn't been a single previous game about trading jewels (or collecting sets of drafted cards, for that matter) so cleanly cut down to its bare essentials as Jaipur. Bonuses aside (which give the game some welcome unpredictability), the game has no additives: no exceptions, no extra mechanics to fit the topic, no strange conditions... 

Even though players expert enough to know when to do what might complain about some luck of the draw, Jaipur is a reliable, definitely recommendable game: it plays very quickly, it's flexible and highly tactical - often enough collecting and selling jewels at the right pace is more important than gathering loads of jewels or camels at once and leave therefore lots of tasty cards up for grabs.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Gin Rummy with the dial turned up to 11, Jaipur is a gloriously-honed game which almost sings 'well playtested' at you when it comes out of the box. Extraordinary balance worthy of the hype, excellent give-and-take interaction, and some off-key art which well befits the theme. First class!

Further Thoughts: This one is starting to lose its shine a little and I'm not as compelled to play it as once I was. There's a bit too much counting, not enough variety and the longevity isn't what it first promised.

齋浦爾商人
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Your turn is to collect cards (6 different colours of various raritude) from the 5 card draft, or sell cards from your hand for VP's. I think the camel idea it implements is clever here - instead of the usual pick 1 card (very staid), you have the option of picking all the camel cards in the draft (they don't get assessed against your hand limit), and then on a later turn exchanging as many as you like with cards in the display, quickly increasing your hand. That leaves a bunch of camel cards in the draft, which the other player may now decide to acquire for later, and the wheel turns. The game also creates pressure to sell which is nice - sell early and you get the best VP's, but hold onto them and trade 3, 4 or 5 at a time and you'll get bonus points, Thurn And Taxis style. The 7 card hand limit also drives the selling. The game is pleasant to play and seemingly spouse friendly. Turns roll along, no big or hard decisions, picking up for a few turns in a row, selling cards for VPs, then back to picking up, but always being aware of what the other player is doing to improve your own decision making on what to pick up and how. Like Fjords, you may want to play only 1 short hand rather than the rules-driven first to win 2 out of 3, but like Lost Cities you probably need to play the extra hands to help balance out the luck of the draft draw. Funnily enough my first playing of this was as a three player where it played similarly and worked fine. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A truly elegant solution to a problem I didn't even realise existed: "How to design a 2 player trading game".
The agony of choice as to what to take from the central market and when does not appear to be all that compelling at first blush but I cannot tell you how many times I have stared skyward at my own stupidity while being schooled by my wife. It is a delicious problem with an almost push your luck quality to it: do I snap up all those camels and give myself all that extra trading power while offering unknown and possibly very valuable cards to my opponent? Do I force the choice onto them by selling my set short? They seem to be collecting silk, do I really want to add that back into the market?
One of if not the best solely 2 player card game available today.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very cool trading game. Girlfriend loves it and requests it. One of the best, light 2P game I've played.

Deeper than appears. We have used some bizarro strategies in this one. If way behind, you can take some wild chances too and sometimes it works out. Sometimes, not so much...

Update 6/12 - I think the 3P variant is SOLID. Deal out 4 cards each to start with & the round ends when 4 piles (VS 3) of goods being gone. Shuffle discards back in if needed. 

**Really want to try this 4P partnership variant I found posted here on BGG:
"Prepare four "passing tokens" marked with "P" or "Pass" on one side and blank on the other. Place one with the "P" side down before each player. Partners sit opposite each other. Deal three cards to each player and start the market with only two camels plus three random cards from the deck.

As long as his passing token shows its blank side, the active player may, rather than buy or sell goods or take camels, pass one goods card to his partner, who then takes it into his hand and passes a goods card back. Camels may not be passed. The player receiving the card must have at least one goods card in his hand. The player sending the card then turns his passing token to its "P" side. This action takes the passing player's full turn. No player may pass cards on two successive turns. On the player's next turn he flips the token to its blank side.

The partnership which has the most camels when their camel card counts are added together wins the camel token. At the end of the round each partnership scores the value of their combined tokens. The partnership winning two rounds wins the game

Tactical note: A player who passes a card will often choose the most valuable single card he holds (at the beginning) or a card which he has seen his partner collect (later in the game). The player receiving a card from his partner should not pass the same card back unless it would hurt his hand to keep it."

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Great quick little 2-player game. Occupies the same niche as Lost Cities, for instance.

The rules are very simple; you take turns taking one of four possible actions (take a good card, take all the camel cards, exchange goods/camels for multiple good cards, sell a set of goods for points) until the game ends. Most points wins. You basically try to collect a lot of the same good, preferably the most valuable goods, preferably before your opponent does.

The clever bit, which might not be apparent from just reading the rules, is that everything you do influences what your opponent can do on their turn. You aren't just collecting sets and scoring points, you have to deny your opponent opportunities at the same time. Timing is critical.

For instance, collecting large sets gives more points, but being the first to turn in a certain set scores more points too. Therefore, the optimum moment to turn in a certain set is the turn before your opponent does.

If you take all the camels, that means a lot of new good cards will be turned over for your opponent to take. If you exchange a lot of camels, there will be a lot of camels for your opponent to take. If you are at your maximum of 7 hand cards, you can't take a single good. If you have no goods or camels to exchange, you can't take multiple goods. None of this is necessarily a big problem, if you time it just so that your opponent can't make the most use of it.

Timing, managing your hand, thinking a turn or two ahead, counting cards, deducing what your opponent has in their hand... there's a lot going on in this simple set collection game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Update: Yeah I like the quick pace and how the camels affect the market, hopefully in your favour. Still it is too much number crunching.

One session and because of the theme and trading card play, I'm thinking this is a travel version of Samarkand! 

Prior to this game I played Court of the Medici which help bring into sharp relief why I enjoyed Jaipur more. Despite the latter design having more rules, there were redeeming features: vp chips for traded cards indicated remaining distribution, so reducing the burden of card counting; vp chips decreased in value when traded for, so there is a race element and a feel of who could be leading, so no number crunching (though not accurate as there are random bonus vp chips for trading large sets); the camels create an interesting majority race/clean up of the draft. So it is an accessible, tension-induced design with uncertainties thrown in, so I like it.

RATING: 8
[floatleft][ImageID=985140][/floatleft]Jaipur is the game that should be recommended for two players instead of games like Lost Cities or Jambo. Jaipur is excellent. The rules are extremely acute. Jaipur has strategic value beyond just drawing and selling cards. Players must balance between selling cards immediately for a quick gain or hold out for larger reward. Players must manage their hands effectively while utilizing the camels to help change out cards quickly and efficiently.

My biggest gripe with the game is setup. Setup is a pain but it does not detract from the game itself. It's just annoying. 

Jaipur contains a slight luck element but it is mitigated by the camels allowing you to manage your hand with some efficiency.

UPDATE: 2012-08-06

In my wishlist comment, I said "Looks like a fun, quick game. Does it have enough replayability?" and that concern has forced us to shelve the game for a while out of fear of overplaying it. Fortunately, since picking up Jaipur we've discovered quite a few fun and quick 2 player games to fill the same niche.

Dropping from a 9 to an 8.

RATING: 9.33
Bonito, simples e contagiante, é mais um jogo sobre camelos e também ele fenomenal. Tínhamos “Através do Deserto”, “Oásis” e “Yspahan”, agora temos também “Jaipur”, distinto de todos por ser unicamente para dois jogadores. Basicamente é um cardgame que gira em torno da mecânica tradicional, set collection, contudo, este é mais colorido do que de costume e tem adornos de jogo de tabuleiro – dezenas de chips, na boa moda do poker! Na verdade é um jogo de timing e onde temos de controlar o que o nosso adversário tem na mão, tarefa não tão difícil quanto parece, pois a única coisa que não sabemos são as 5 cartas iniciais, já que todo o resto vem da mesa. Obrigatório!

RATING: 8
Lovely game. Played the app to death (the the point where I'm burnt out on it) so that says something. Would be even better of some of the app's variants also came in the box.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Played once at Glasgow Games Festival (2014):

Selected at random as there were only two of us and there were no bigger games we could have jumped into.

From the get-go we realised we had a gem, rules are clear and the rule book is very well laid out.

Before we even took our first turn both of us were going "aha" several times as the game mechanics started to make sense and the amount of decisions and their consequences started to unfold.. this is before we even took our first turn!

Everything just makes sense; do you go for the camels and give the other player a refreshed marked place, do you take one item and leave everything else for the other player, do you switch cards, but that doesn't increase your hand size.

"Aha"-moments don't stop at the first turn. After a couple more you realize that you can collect sets or just snipe the top prizes before the other player has finished his combo set. You realize that there is timing to things to allow you to keep momentum; there is a lot happening.

Will buy the game and update this comment with how it plays after several games.

Update:
Now played this game very many times. It is still a very good 2 player game. Even took it with me on a month long road trip (and ended up playing it several times in laundromats). The game started to lose a bit appeal, but recently my 9 year old nephew came to visit and we played the fuck out of this game. He really enjoyed it (and beat me a few times as well) - for that reason I'm leaving the score as it is.

Update 2: 
Played a ton of this with my 9 year old nephew - he loved it. He liked to count the number of points each move made - and analyse the reason one player one over the other ('you sold a lot of low pointers for bonus tiles, rather than wait for the rare cards'). I had to buy him a copy of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of my favorite two-player boardgames. It is simple to learn and simple to play, but there's still a lot of subtlety to the game and it's at least as much about second-guessing the other player as it is about being versed in the mechanics of the game. The opening hand you're dealt is the only truly hidden information, and it sort of starts you in a direction, tactically, but to turn that into a victory you have to adapt to what comes out of the deck better than your opponent does.

The most interesting thing about the game to me is the way that player actions interleave so deeply. You practically always have several actions you want to take, but you only get ONE action per turn. So you're only ever getting to do maybe half of what you want, and the opponent gets her turn before you can do the other half of it. So she might (or might not) do something that disrupts your plan. Skilled players can use this to their advantage. e.g. If you time it right, you can flood the market with camels at a moment when your opponent doesn't want to waste his turn sweeping them, and thereby execute a buy-out for effectively no cost.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
2014: 8. Brilliant little two player game.  There's a lot of interesting and subtle back and forth going on here. Taking one action that's good for you seems to always present an opportunity that your opponent can take advantage of.  Do you fight with them over a particular good, or try to max out on what your opponent is giving away?  Take the camels?  You may turn up something they need.  Use the camels?  You just gave your opponent a chance to take 5 points.

Plays quick with interesting decisions and interactions that require shrewdness and a bit of luck.

Charming art and packaging, small form factor.  A fantastic two player game!

2017: 9. Upgraded, my love has been rekindled with the iOS release, there's some neat game variants in there that can refresh your experience and change your thinking. It's still an excellent 2-player affair for gamers and non-gamers alike.
