Julie Favourite
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[2011-01-23]
Gyönyörű kivitelezésű, kereskedős kétszemélyes játék. A doboz nem háromszor akkora, mint a játék, jól kialakított, pont akkora, amekkorára szükség van. Szépek, jó anyagúak a kártyák és megfelelőek a zsetonok is.
A játékosok megpróbálják a hat különböző árucikket a legjobb módon eladni a kezükből. Ezeket különböző módon gyűjtjük, majd jó időben beváltjuk. Ehhez jön az ötletes tevekártya megvalósítás. A játékban több helyen is megjelenik a szerencse, maguknak a kártyáknak a feljövetelekor, illetve a bónuszzsetonok megszerzésekor, mégis úgy érzi az ember, hogy nem számítanak ezek nagy mértékben. A pontszámok elég közel alakulnak egymáshoz a partik végén és kettő győzelem kell a végső győztes kihirdetéséhez.
Kellemes, gyors, könnyed, mégis stratégiára és sok taktikára lehetőséget biztosító kártyajátékról van szó. A konfliktus mértéke igen csekély, így pároknak is bátran ajánlható.

RATING: 7
Nice little two player trading game.  It's a cute race for sets to cash in.  Mostly harmless, but does most everything right.

I will say that this game commits one of my biggest pet peeves.  It plays as a nice simple quick 10 minute game, but then the rules ask you to play the game over again to get best 2 out of 3.  What's wrong with just seeing who wins and if you like it you play again.  I hate when the rules "require" you to play the same game again with no growth or meaning from the previous game.  Knizia established this play the game three times thing years ago, and I think designers really need to get over it.

RATING: 6.5
A nice, quick-playing set collection game. There are six types of goods, and camels (which help you accumulate goods more easily from the market). You take goods from market, or trade for them, trying to get sets which you sell for money: for each type of good, earlier sales are better (mostly), and you also get a bonus for larger sets. Each round, the player who made the most money gets a seal, then repeat. The first player to win 2 seals wins the game.

RATING: 9
One of the best games for two I've played in a while.  Every turn has meaningful decisions thanks to the elegant design. The seven card hand limit, the camels, bonus chips for collecting sets of three or more, all add to the delicious tension.  Easy to learn and the chips are a nice touch.  Lost Cities is one of my wife's favorites and this is close enough in style that it should appeal to her too.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of my favorite small, quick, two-player games.

This game for two players only, has players competing as merchants to make the most rupees in a round and the winner is whoever wins 2 out of 3 rounds.  The game consists of a deck of cards and pile of tokens and the gameplay is really simple.  On a player's turn they can collect or sell goods.  Collecting goods is broken down into either picking up a single good, picking up multiples but having to give up an equal number of goods or camels in your herd (the player with the largest herd at the end of a round gets 5 extra rupees), or picking up all the camels in the market.

The earlier you sell any specific goods the more rupees you will get and selling larger numbers at once gets you a rupee bonus.  A round ends when either the deck of cards is empty or 3 of the five goods tiles are exhausted.

I think this game has endless charm, a lot of interesting decisions to be made, and is quick and simple enough to get it up and running even with new players.  Great option for two players looking for something a little lighter and quicker.

RATING: 7
Lovely game.

Online I prefer to play it in the "open information" variant used at yucata.de, where all information that has once been revealed stays open. It makes for a more tactical game when you have a better idea of what your opponent has on hand, and it also makes it strategically attractive to not sell or exchange all your starting hand cards, as they are the only cards hidden from your opponent, and having hidden cards is a significant strategic advantage.

It's possible to play this way with the physical copy as well, but it makes it a bit fiddler to remember the hand size limit of 7. I did play that way a few times when I first got the physical copy, but now I only play the regular version when playing with a physical copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[Gameworks 2009 edition] {couple, Arabian; hand-management}  Jaipus is a very quick and handy 2p game.  It is highly accessible for new players.  The design is elegant and choices of action are simple - trade or sell cards each turn; however, players have to smartly decide the right timing to sell (before the opponents, but still can good scores), what to trade, or what to take.  Smart game - the tension last through the whole game.  A player usually cannot confirm whether he wins/loses the win until scoring.  Jaipur is easy to learn and teach and plays quickly (less than 30 mins per game)

The quality of game components are nice, similar to previous GameWorks' games.  The illustration is colorful.  I prefer thicker tokens, as in Animalia.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Two player set collection card game with a trading theme. Players need to accumulate matching cards in order to obtain tokens worth victory points. This is done by either adding one card to your hand, swapping already held cards with displayed draw cards, or using a certain number of camel cards to replace displayed cards. Once a certain number of matching cards have been built up, tokens depicting the card's good are acquired. This is a relatively simple game and there is quite a lot of luck involved due to which cards are available on your turn. There are strategic choices here though, such as how many camel cards to build up, giving yourself more flexibility when selecting goods cards, and monitoring how many cards your opponent has at any one time, which should inform your choices on which cards to leave in play. Beyond that though there isn't a lot here to propel 'Jaipur' beyond other similar set collection games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Laura's Rank (4/15) – 3.5 out of 5. Pros: The mechanic is built for two players, so it is great with two players. It is easy to learn. The artwork and colors are good. It's a quick game as well. Cons: This game appears strategy heavy, but there is a decent amount of luck involved. This probably means that it is designed well, but I can get frustrated because it can thwart my plans pretty often.

Ryan's Rank (4/15) – 4 out of 5. Most of the games I play are just with Laura and I, so this is a nice two player game to have in our collection. I like the light strategy. I like the decisions that have to be made about whether to sell or hold on until you have more items to get a bonus. Just an all-around good game.

Overall Rank (4/15) – 7.5 out of 10

RATING: 7.5
A short, tense little game for two that can be very competitive, but somehow manages to not feel too mean. It usually doesn't benefit you to constantly take what the other person is going for since you'll just both end up with reduced profits, so you'd really only want to do it to prevent them from getting a huge combo and running away with the game. It's a card game, so, by nature, prone to randomness and luck, but in the games I've played, I didn't feel crippled when card draws didn't go the way I wanted - it really just forced me to change my strategy, and winning still seemed possible. I like this game a lot!

RATING: 8

RATING: 6
I love card games, especially when they manage to accomplish a lot with very little. Games like Lost Cities (old) and Linko (new) come to mind when I think about designer cards games that give me great pleasure. What I am less enthusiastic about are games that rely on heavy combo-building to deliver complex decisions. Building combinations, as in games like Race for the Galaxy, Dominion, and Jambo, can be fun and rewarding, but they also feel baroque -- maybe even forced.

Jaipur, despite coming out in 2009 at the height of a combo-building craze, eschews the technique in favor of straightforward hand management and timing. That's not to say, however, that the game is straightforward, simple, or easy. What it means is that the game has a certain elegance that I appreciate

On your turn, you either take cards (in an attempt to build up sets) or sell (i.e., "meld") cards. The bigger the set, the more points you earn. Best of three hands wins the overall game. Easy! But no so fast. The way you take cards matters. You can take a single card, sure, but you can also take multiple cards if you are willing to trade them for cards already in your hand. Do that, however, and you might deliver a boon to your opponent, so be careful. And then there are camel cards. They seem like freebies, but in reality they add just enough chaos to make them feel risky after all.

Jaipur is fun and pleasant. The decisions won't rack your brain, but there is enough subtlety to the timing of your card play to make the game worthwhile. You can play Jaipur as a diversion, or you can buckle down for a little bit of hard-won strategy. The choice is yours.

After just one round, my sister said, "A little fiddly, and not enough oomph."  She's right.  Jaipur is beautifully produced, right down to the vivid artwork and sculpted box insert.  But sorting and stacking the 50+ beautiful bonus disks is a bummer before each round.  That would be fine if the game were substantial, but it's a fluffy "best of three" affair with little tension and few difficult choices.  If the players are evenly matched, you'll be stacking those bonus disks three times.

RATING: 7
The game was a breeze to teach. The rules are laid out incredibly well for a  visual learner like me. It took me literally 5 minutes with the rules to know the game perfectly. 

We finished two rounds (and the whole game, because I won both) in a little over 30 minutes. This will speed up for sure when not restrained by space and not learning it. 

It's also very pretty. Ash liked the pink insert. She's been to Jaipur and has fond memories of the all-pink architecture. A+ on the artwork and component quality. 

It will get more tense as we learn the distribution of cards and other strategies. I can see a lot of depth for such simple rules, which is a big plus for 2p games with my wife 

Overall, I like it. I could see it becoming one of my favorite small, 2p games. It's not as good as [thing=760]Battle Line[/thing], but I'm certain I'll never get rid of it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Fun at first, but I don't think it will wear well.  We'll see.

The decisions are often fairly easy, but mildly interesting, which makes the game pleasant enough. Then once per hand you are truly agonizing over the proper course and that makes for a tasty treat. Then once per game your opponent pretty much hoses you and that makes it funny.

I have no idea why this is ever compared to Jambo.  They don't seem anything alike to me. 

Update: Huh. I played this again recently and I liked it. 

No it still isn't as much fun as Jambo, but, then again, it is not as much fun as eating sushi, sex or winning stuff. Three other things that are not at all like Jaipur.

Update: I am sort of finding this to be one of my two fallback games (Longhorn being the other) for a quick introductory game for non-gamers, a quick finisher or a one last game before bed.




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 5
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 3
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 1
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 5


Overall - 3.75

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Jaipur is a smooth, fast 2 player trading game with a stunning presentation,  right down to the inside of the game box.

A light entry with very simple rules,  it is primarily an open information game,  whereby you try to top your rival in a best two out of three affair.

It is all about choosing the right time to sell your goods to the market,  while trying to earn bonuses along the way.  Besides the goods,  there are camels in the game, which are sort of a balancing feature,  that provide you with additional flexibility.  There is also a 5 point end game bonus for the player who has collected the most camels,  which may be enough to get you over the hump.

Overall,  Jaipur is a pleasant journey that is easy on the eyes and easy on the gray cells.  Just be aware,  that as far as the role luck plays in the game,  consider it heavily laced.     
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
If you enjoy collecting money for playing cards rummy style, this is your game. Wife and I always love bringing this game out. Easy to learn and doesn't take long to play.

Buy cards, play multiples of the same type to gain coins and bonuses for runs of 3, 4, or 5 of a kind. Whoever has the most money at the end wins. Simple and fun. If you are looking for a great 2 player time killer without the highly competitive, cut-throat gameplay, go with Jaipur!

RATING: 7
Sort of scratches the Jambo itch but plays much faster and revolves around a simpler set collection race to get the most valuable tokens. Game would be far too fast without the best of three rounds addition.

Players attempt to collect sets of goods cards. Each turn consists of either selling 1 or more cards of a type or collecting more cards. Selling goods gets you tokens, selling large sets gives you bonus tokens as well. Most of the goods decrease in value as more is sold adding a race-like tension to the game.

Some very cute mechanics around the camel cards that interact nicely with the hand limit in getting cards. Nicely balanced and player controllable round-end condition also. Easy to explain.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[thing=54043][/thing] is a set collection with hand management game for two players. In a player turn the possible actions are:
- draw one card for your hand (limit of 7 cards in hand);
- draw two or more cards, in this case you must trade for other different good cards you have in you hand and/or camels;
- take all the camel cards;
- sell goods.

There are six types of goods: diamonds (6), gold (6) and silver (6) are the ones that are worth more points, but there are less of them in the deck. These goods can only be sold by using two or more cards of its type. Cloth (8), spice (8) and leather (10) are worth less, but there are more of them and they can be sold even by trading in one card.

When a player sell 3, 4 or 5 cards of the same good at the same time, he gains bonus tokens corresponding with the number of cards sold (so, if you sell 3 goods, you take the bonus chip with 3 cards showing on it) - the bonus differ in the amount of VP they give: 1, 2 or 3 money for the 3 cards chips; 4, 5 or 6 for the 4 cards chips; and 8, 9 or 10 money for the 5 cards chips. You will only know the amount of money gained when you take the chip. The chips of your opponent remain hidden until the end of the round.

[thing=54043][/thing] is played up to 3 rounds, and each round ends when: the draw deck runs out, or three stack of chips of different goods are empty. The winner of the round will be the player with the most money. The winner of the game will be the one that wins 2 rounds.

I found [thing=54043][/thing] to be an enjoyable, allowing much with so little: the options aren't overwhelming, but they permit you to do some fine plays. Most of them involving the camels. The camels, to me, are one of the cores of [thing=54043][/thing], one of the things that makes it better than, say, [thing=31105][/thing]. Because, if you have two gold cards in your hand, a a gold comes up in the market, you will probably just take it, if able. No mistery, no real thought behind: you just want more because more is, normally, better, specially if it is a high value good. But with the camels, well, you have so many more options. You can take the gold AND one or more cards you think (or know) the other players really wants. And the camels will be there, blocking other cards to show up, until someone takes them. Also, if the other player takes the camels, yes, he can gain 5 points (if in the end of the round he has more camels than you), but he also opens up several new cards for you! Having camels puts pressure in the other player, because he knows you can just take a high number of cards, without giving him anything new, except, well, camels. He may take them, making the camel balance tilt to his side, but at what cost?

The second thing that makes [thing=54043][/thing] shine? Timming. Again, in [thing=54043][/thing] you have a mechanism (the sand storm) that puts some pressure to sell goods, puting the better reward x losing big in the table. But the sand storm hits everyone, and it also kind that makes the game slower, since once you lose goods to the sand storm, you won't be able to take them all back, because you have to trade for them. Also, in [thing=50][/thing], the pressure only comes when the deck is running out and you still have a lot of cards to put down - so, timming is important in [thing=50][/thing], but it only comes from the deck.

In [thing=54043][/thing] the pressure comes from the deck, but also from the other side of the table. A player can make the game go faster by trading quickly, and this also have another reason: the first chips of the goods (except silver) are worth more, so the first that sell them, will get more. So, if I believe that you have several green cards, I can sell a couple of them only to take the 5 and 3 money chips. This makes the whole experience richer and tenser (and this sort of "race" is helped by the camels, since you can take several cards of the same type in one swoop, and sell them in your next turn).

Playing three rounds of [thing=54043][/thing] I guess last around the same as three plays of [thing=50][/thing] - but, I have only played on Yucata, so I can't say for sure (for this same reason, I can't talk about the quality of the components). A friends that has an actual copy of the game said the it lasts around 30-45 minutes.

Overall, [thing=54043][/thing] is a solid, portable and smart game, fitting pretty well in its two player slot, since it is confrontational, but only in an indirect way (taking what the other player wants, selling before the other do it), which is good when you don't want to get mad at each other over a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of my favorite 2 player games.  (Possibly also top 10 overall?)  The rules are spelled out clearly, which puts it a step above some other games we've bought recently.   It plays quickly and can be adjusted up or down based on how many rounds you want to play.  10-15 minutes is usually enough to finish a round, 30-45 for a full game of 3 rounds.

The components are all surprisingly high quality for coming in in a little box.  The coins come already punched out and the cards have a nice texture to them.  Art is also nice.  I really don't have any complaints about this game at all.  The price point seems a little high for the box size, but once you open it you realize it contains just as many goodies as games that come in boxes twice the size.

It's a fairly light game, so might not be for people who only like heavy games.  However, I find it has enough strategy to keep me interested the whole game.  There's a lot of decision-making going on in regards to what you're going to sell and when.  Do you wait to get 4-5 cards for a bigger payoff, or cash in at 2-3 so your opponent doesn't beat you to it?  Despite the strategy involved, even very analytical players don't turtle around too much.  It's a great gateway game hovering right around Splendor for me.

RATING: 8
Quick two-player game. We enjoyed it very much. As I was just planning my (real) trip to Jaipur it gave it an extra atmosphere :)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Jaipur, capital of Rajasthan. You are one of the two most powerful traders in the city. But that's not enough for you, because only the merchant with two Seals of Excellence will have the privilege of being invited to the Maharaja's court. You are therefore going to have to do better than your direct competitor by buying, exchanging and selling at better prices, all while keeping an eye on both your camel herds.
\13\\13\
When it's your turn, you can either take or sell cards.
\13\\13\
If you take cards, you have to chose between taking all the camels, taking 1 card from the market or swapping 2 to 5 cards between the market and your cards.
\13\\13\
If you sell cards, you get to sell only one type of good per turn, and you get as many chips from that good as you sold cards. The chips' values decrease as the game progresses, so you'd better hurry ! But, on the other hand, you get increasingly high rewards for selling 3, 4, or 5 cards of the same good at a time, so you'd better wait !
\13\\13\
You can't sell camels, but they're paramount for trading and they're also worth a little something at the end of the round, enough sometimes to secure the win, so you have to use them smartly.
\13\\13\
Jaipur is a fast-paced card game, a blend of tactics, risk and luck.

RATING: 7.5
Jaipur is a very neat two-player game and one that I enjoy.  The rules are very simple but their are some different strategies to take and players really need to watch their opponent closely.  So far, games have always come very close (usually within a few points) and the game is very tight and tense because it's hard to truly know who's in the lead.  This tension is part of what makes Jaipur so much fun as you feel like you're racing neck-and-neck with your opponent for points up until the very end.

Highly recommended as a fun, quick game for two.

This is one of the "go to games" for me and my wife.  It is quick and easy to set up.  It gives a bit of strategy and a bit of luck.  Nice and light, good for after a day of work or taking care of the kids.  A game that you can talk about the day while playing without it being detrimental to the play.  Plays in 10-15 mins/round and you can play as many rounds as you like to determine a winner (or just play each round as a game and have multiple winners).  It has some interaction via the shared pool of cards, but doesn't have the more painful types of interaction such as Archaeology: The Card Game, one of our other "go to games" with a similar feel (time requirement, luck vs strategy, simple mechanics, etc.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've never won a game.

Ever.

What makes this game so wonderful is it's ability to make you believe, "I've got a chance...one more gold and I'm..umm..golden!"

Luck is imbedded into this game, but skill also plays a part.  Hence my 0-23 game losing streak.  Yeah. Sometimes I want to just hop on my camel and bounce my way home...

I'll play this game any where, any time.  If I hear, "Jaipur" in a crowded room, I'll 'Marco Polo' that voice until I find the body attached to it.  Five minutes later on a section of buffet table that I cleared, camels WILL be traded.  :)

Quick set-up, gorgeous artwork on the cards, beautiful display of coins and goods, a tactile experience that soothes the soul, and set collection.  Everything I love in a game.  I'm sighing with delight even as I write.

Because of the luck factor, there can be wild swings of play.  There are also multiple rounds in a game.  So if one round goes badly for you, shuffle-up, deal-up and trade some more.  Just don't take my camel stowing the cute panda bear!

Re-playability is medium if you play with the same person constantly, but high with a variety of people.  Every one has a slightly different strategy.  I've learned mine has a 'desert' quality.  Where's my camel?...

RATING: 6
Quite a straightforward game, from the designer of one of my favourite games, Yspahan. This one isn't as good as I had hoped, but it is quite fun.

It's fairly light and quick, with simple rules and only a few types of cards. The tactics lie in timing your buying and selling so as to get the jump on your opponent, and in ending a round when it's to your advantage to do so.

The supposed play time of 30 minutes, though, only seems accurate if the game ends after 2 rounds. If it goes to a third (decider), it takes closer to 45 in our experience. In other words, it's about 15 minutes per round.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is great. It combines set collection and buying/selling to make a great little card game. Timing is also very important as you want to sell as much as you can before the piles run out so order is important. I liked the use of the camels too, it was mildly thematic. 

As a 2-player filler/light game, this is in a league of its own. It's almost too bad it's only for 2 since it limits the playtime for us.

RATING: 9
This is an enjoyable 2-player family game. It feels like a mix between a light euro and a classic card game. It simulates trading at a market in a very cool manner, which is something hard to simulate with just two players. The game features some difficult decisions, such as when to pick up a valuable card vs a couple camels, how long do you dare hold on to cards trying to increase your sell bonus, etc. This seems a good couples game, but having a non-gaming spouse I cannot confirm this. :)

As with all GameWorks titles, this one is graphically very pleasing, features quality components and a box sized for them, and as always the insert is stunning.

RATING: 7
One of the better 2-player games I have played.  I liked it enough to teach it to several other people when we're just looking for a quick filler.  I enjoyed the tension between the hand management and the decision to sell now or hold on for a bigger set.  Of course the outcome depends largely on how the cards come out, but it can be mitigated by careful manipulation of the display.  Still, there will be times when things just don't go your way and everything you want ends up getting snatched up by your opponent first.  Jaipur isn't very deep, but I don't believe it's completely devoid of meaningful choices either.
